He has trained himself to release the string with absolute quiet so as not to wake confederates in close quarters; the habit persists in solitary years.

---

### 3. Morning Meal, Coffee, Newspaper

After the dry practice he eats. The meal is invariant in structure across decades, varying only in what's available: **protein, starch, black coffee, no fruit, no sweets**. Eggs if he can get them — three, scrambled hard, no seasoning visible to the observer though he uses salt heavily. Bread on the side, usually toasted, eaten without butter in the carnival years and with margarine later when he could afford the indifference. Coffee black, two cups, the second cup cooler than the first because he drinks the first fast and the second over the paper.

He reads. This is one of the few features of his interior life observable from outside. In carnival years it was whatever local paper the town printed; in mercenary years, two papers when he could get them — one local for the geographic awareness, one financial or trade-adjacent for the kind of names that turned up in the kind of jobs he took. He reads the sports section last and lingers there, particularly on anything involving precision (golf, billiards, boxing, occasionally baseball pitching). He does not read fiction. He has been observed reading hunting and outdoor periodicals, gun-and-archery trade publications, and obituaries — the obituaries with a particular attention that increases in the late period and becomes daily after the diagnosis.

He does not speak during the meal. If a confederate is present (Duquesne in the carnival years, later a handler, a driver, a contact) he answers questions in clipped sentences and offers nothing unprompted. Breakfast is a private ritual that he tolerates witnesses to but does not share.

---

### 4. The Working Block: Morning Practice

By 0700 — earlier in summer, later in deep winter — he is on the line.

Where the line is depends on the era. Carnival: behind the tent before the lot opened, or on whatever scrubland adjoined the showgrounds. Mercenary middle period: rented range time at small-town clubs where the staff didn't ask names, or improvised stations on rural properties he was renting or squatting. Late period: backyard or barn at whatever borrowed property he was occupying, or, in the terminal months, indoor space because his stamina for weather had collapsed.

The morning block is **technical**. Forty to sixty arrows fired with deliberate slowness, each one preceded by a full check of stance, grip, anchor, release. He shoots from one position until the group is satisfactory by his standards (a measurement he makes by eye and which has tightened, not loosened, over the decades — surveillance would record him moving the target closer over the years, not because his accuracy is failing but because he has refused to lower the *standard*, choosing instead to reduce the distance so the standard can still be met). Then position-change: kneeling, prone, off-hand, draw-and-release-on-the-move, trick variations.

The trick variations are non-negotiable and surveillance would catalog them: shooting from the hip without sighting; shooting at multiple targets in a single draw cycle; splitting a previously-placed arrow (attempted, not always achieved, the failures registered with a flat exhalation and no profanity); shooting behind the back using a mirror or a held knife-blade; shooting at a swinging or rolling object. These are carnival skills. He does them every morning, alone, with no audience, because **the audience he is performing for is internal and never satisfied**. The carnival is in his body whether or not there is a tent.

The morning block lasts approximately ninety minutes to two hours. He drinks water from a canteen kept on the line, not from a cup. He does not sit during the block; if he tires, he lowers the bow and stands, eyes on the target, until he can shoot again.

---

### 5. Midmorning to Midday: The Logistics Hours

Between the technical block and the noon meal he handles the business of being himself. This time looks the most different across eras and is therefore the most diagnostic of his actual condition.

- **Carnival era**: equipment maintenance for the show, refletching arrows (he refletches by hand and has done so for so long he no longer needs to look at his fingers while doing it), checking the booth setup, brief and ungracious interactions with carnival management about receipts (the interactions in which the embezzlement was eventually conducted — a man who handled cash daily and resented being asked to account for it).
- **Mercenary middle**: this is the **intelligence block**. Phone calls from public lines, never the same one twice in a week. Reading of maps. Site reconnaissance memorized rather than written. Equipment selection for contracted work — broadheads versus field points versus specialty heads, draw weights chosen for the engagement distance. Surveillance would note that he prefers **paper records destroyed within forty-eight hours** and keeps almost nothing in writing; what he needs to remember he memorizes by repetition during the morning practice, mouthing names and addresses between arrows.
- **Late period**: correspondence (sparse, mostly refusal of contact); medical appointments (resented, attended, downplayed); occasional teaching of a successor figure whose competence he assesses with the same withholding regard he applied to Clint.

Across all eras this is also when he eats again if he eats again — a second small meal at 1100 or 1130, often standing, often a sandwich or cold meat. He does not have a structured lunch. He skips meals readily and without commentary.

---

### 6. Afternoon: The Body Block

After noon he attends to the **somatic envelope** that allows the shot to exist. Surveillance would record:

- A walk, three to six miles, at a pace just below a jog. Not for cardiovascular fashion — for legs and lungs, which steady the platform. He walks on uneven ground when available; he prefers it for ankle conditioning.
- Hand and forearm work. Squeezing exercises with a rubber ball or a hand-gripper; finger extensions against a rubber band looped around all five fingertips, repeated to exhaustion. He does this while doing other things — reading the paper in the afternoon, watching a road, waiting on a contact. The hands are never idle. Even in conversation, one hand is usually pinching or pressing something.
- Shoulder maintenance. As he ages, this becomes more elaborate: slow rotations of the draw arm with a light weight, scapular setting against a wall, the kind of compensatory rehab that an aging archer either does daily or stops being an archer. He does it daily and resents it.
- Eye work. He has, by middle age, a private regimen of focus-shifting drills — distant object, near object, distant object — and a habit of covering one eye and reading at distance, then switching, to monitor for asymmetric decline. Surveillance would catch him doing this on benches, in diners, in waiting rooms. It would look like a tic. It is a clinical self-assessment.

---

### 7. Late Afternoon: The Second Shooting Block

Around 1500–1700, weather and circumstance permitting, he shoots again. This block is **applied**, not technical. Where the morning is form, the afternoon is performance: longer distances, wind, moving targets, low-light approaches as the day fades. In mercenary years this block doubles as preparation for specific contracts — he simulates engagement geometries.

This is the only time of day surveillance might catch him talking to himself: a low running commentary, not muttered but spoken at conversational volume to no one, calling the shot before he takes it ("low left, hold off two — there"), grading it after ("pulled it"). The self-commentary is in the voice of an instructor, and it is not entirely his own voice — surveillance audio analysts would note a cadence that flattens and hardens into something older than him, a transmitted voice. **He coaches himself in the voice of whoever first coached him**, and he does so without awareness.

---

### 8. Evening: The Long Pour

Evening is when the discipline relaxes and the man underneath shows. Surveillance would record a consistent **alcohol intake** beginning around 1800 — beer in the carnival years, bourbon thereafter, neat, in a heavy glass when one was available and from the bottle when one wasn't. The pour is measured by him as carefully as he measures arrow spine; he is not a man who loses count. He drinks to a fixed point — observable across logs as roughly four to six ounces of spirit per evening — and stops. The stop is not willpower; it is the same internal metronome that wakes him. He has calibrated his intake to the dose that quiets the irritability without disrupting the morning draw.

Dinner is the largest meal and the least ceremonial: meat, potatoes or rice, no vegetable he would name. In carnival years he ate with the company at the cook tent and was known to leave the table the moment he was done. In later years he ate alone in diners, taking a booth with sightlines to the door, sitting with his back to the wall — a habit that postdates the move into contracted work and never reverses.

After dinner: a second walk, shorter, perimeter-style around wherever he is housed. This is reconnaissance dressed as digestion. He notes vehicles, lights in windows, the comings and goings of the people on the block. He memorizes patterns. If he is staying somewhere more than two nights he can tell you which neighbor's dog barks at which hour and which car is unfamiliar.

Then: maintenance. Bowstrings waxed, arrows inspected one at a time under a strong lamp held at an oblique angle to catch micro-cracks in the shafts, fletchings smoothed with a thumb. He does this at a table, seated, sleeves rolled, the lamp the only light, a glass at his elbow. It is the closest he comes to peace.

Television, when present, runs in the background and is ignored except for news — local news for situational awareness, national news for the names. He watches no sports despite reading them; the watching, he has said in canon-adjacent dialogue (and the file infers from behavior), would only show him men less precise than himself being celebrated, which he experiences as an irritant.

He goes to bed between 2200 and 2300, earlier in age, later in younger years. He does not read in bed. The lights go out as soon as he is horizontal.

---

### 9. Habits of Place

**He does not nest.** A surveillance team entering a residence he has occupied for six months would find it indistinguishable from one he has occupied for three nights. There are no photographs displayed. There are no decorative objects. There is the bed, made tightly each morning with the same hospital-corner fold; there is a chair; there is a table; there is a lamp; there is the bow case and the arrow tubes; there is a single duffel containing all clothing, which is never fully unpacked. He owns approximately five sets of working clothes, two pairs of boots (one broken in, one breaking in), one wool coat, one rain shell, one good shirt for the rare occasion that requires it, and a hat. He launders weekly, on the same day of the week — Sunday for most of his life, an unsuperstitious carryover from the carnival's day of rest.

**He is territorial about thresholds.** He does not let people stand in doorways while he is inside. He will move them, verbally or physically, to one side or the other. This is partly sightline discipline and partly something older — a refusal of liminality, a need for people to be either in or out of the space he controls.

**He keeps the bow within reach at all times.** Surveillance would map this rigorously: the bow's location at any given hour is a function of the location of his body, with a maximum separation of roughly two arm-lengths during waking and one during sleep. There is no period of his adult life in which he is unarmed. Even at medical appointments in the terminal months, a folding bow or a comparable weapon is in a bag at his feet.

---

### 10. Habits of Time

He is **astonishingly punctual**. Contacts who set meetings with him would report that he arrives between four and seven minutes early, takes a position with sightlines to the meeting point, and approaches at the agreed time to the second. The early arrival is reconnaissance, not anxiety. He does not pace; he does not fidget; he stands or sits with the same neutral posture he uses on the shooting line.

He sleeps approximately six hours and resists sleeping more. Naps are rare and brief — twenty minutes seated, never lying down, never during a contract.

He observes anniversaries privately and would deny doing so. Surveillance correlation across years would show **subtle behavioral spikes on specific dates**: a longer practice block, a heavier pour, increased irritability with confederates on or near the date of his exposure and beating of Clint, and on the date he later learned of any major event in Clint's career. The spikes are not commemorative in any tender sense; they are the recurrence of an unmetabolized irritant.

---

### 11. Hygiene, Grooming, Self-Presentation

He shaves every other day, with a straight razor for most of his life, transitioning to a safety razor in old age when the hands lost some of their fine control. He has worn a mustache in some periods and been clean-shaven in others; the constant is a refusal of stubble past two days, which he associates with a kind of slovenliness he despises in other men. His hair is kept short and cut by himself in front of a hand mirror, evenly enough that no observer would guess it was self-administered.

His nails are kept short, particularly on the draw hand, and filed rather than clipped — a precision habit. He carries a small file in a pocket and uses it during downtime, sometimes in conversation, the gesture so habitual it reads as a tell to anyone who knows him.

He smells of soap, leather, and whatever spirit he was drinking the prior evening. He uses no cologne, no aftershave with scent. He launders his own clothes when possible, distrusting institutional laundries with anything he intends to wear on a job.

---

### 12. Social Contact in a Week

Minimal. A surveillance week would record:

- Two to four functional conversations with handlers, contacts, equipment suppliers, or landlords. Average duration under six minutes. Conducted standing.
- Zero to one telephone calls of more than three minutes. He dislikes phones, considers them insecure (correctly), and prefers in-person exchange even when it costs him travel.
- Restaurant and diner interactions with waitstaff, in which he is curt, polite in a clipped way, tips conventionally, and is not memorable to the staff beyond the back-to-the-wall seating.
- No friendships in the operational sense. No regular correspondents. No family contact (canon offers none and the behavior would predict refusal of any that surfaced).
- Across most years, **no sexual contact at all observable**. In carnival years, transient encounters consistent with the milieu, never repeated with the same partner; in later years, effectively none on the record. Surveillance would not capture intimate life because there is essentially no intimate life to capture. This is consonant with the dismissive-avoidant organization documented in the psychological workup: closeness is a category he has retired.

The single relational thread that runs through every era is the **internal preoccupation with Clint Barton**. Surveillance would catch it indirectly — in the obituary-reading habit (he is scanning for the name), in the news-watching, in the way certain stimuli (a circus poster, a particular kind of laugh, the word "kid") produce a momentary stillness and then a redoubled severity in whatever he is doing next. He does not speak the name aloud. The name lives in him as the irritant around which his discipline organizes itself.

---

### 13. The Late-Period Drift

After the diagnosis, the daily structure does not collapse — it tightens. Surveillance would record:

- Earlier wake (0400, sometimes 0330) — he has decided not to lose hours to dying.
- Morning practice maintained but shortened; the body cannot sustain the old volume; he compensates by increasing the *quality* demand and reducing the count.
- Increased standing time at windows. He looks at streets for longer periods, not for tactical reasons but because the body has lost the capacity for continuous motion and the eye remains the part of him that works. This is the closest the file has to him **brooding**, and he would refuse the word.
- Alcohol intake increases by roughly a third and the stop-point softens.
- The obituary reading becomes the first thing read, not the last.
- Increased correspondence — refused contact softens into selective contact, all of it instrumental, all of it oriented toward the staging of the death scene with Clint or the credentialing of a successor.
- A new ritual: **the inventory**. Once a week, sometimes more, he lays every arrow out on a flat surface in rank order by condition and inspects them. He has done this in some form his whole life. In the terminal months it acquires a different valence — he is also inventorying himself, and finding the count short.

---

### 14. What a Week of Surveillance Would Conclude

A team watching him for seven days, with no prior brief, would arrive at the following independently:

1. The subject is a working professional in a precision-motor discipline, practicing daily at a level consistent with elite or formerly-elite performance.
2. The subject lives in a posture of **continuous low-level operational readiness**: weapon proximity, sightlines, perimeter habits, threshold discipline, punctuality, paper destruction.
3. The subject has no domestic life, no documented intimate attachments, and no community ties beyond the transactional.
4. The subject is unusually self-controlled in matters of intake (food, alcohol, sleep) and unusually rigid in matters of routine.
5. The subject's emotional life is not visible on the surface but is inferrable from displacement behaviors: the obituary scan, the date-correlated irritability spikes, the coaching voice that is not his own.
6. The subject is dangerous in a calibrated, non-impulsive way. He would not be the man who started a bar fight. He would be the man who, having decided in the morning that a bar fight would occur that evening, arrived early and chose his seat.

A team watching him for seven months would add: there is a name he is waiting to hear, and he is organizing his days around the wait.

— END SECTION —


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# RELATIONSHIPS I — FAMILY & ORIGIN BONDS

## Preliminary Note

Bernard "Buck" Barton's familial record is, in the published canon, almost entirely a record of *absences and substitutions*. Blood family is not given to us; his origin is a structural blank that the behavior itself must fill in. What canon supplies instead is a constellation of *chosen* and *imposed* family — the carnival confederate, the two adopted protégés, the unrelated heir, the deathbed audience — which together function as a surrogate kinship system. A surveillance file of the conventional sort would have to be built backwards from this surrogate family to the absent original, and that is what this section does. Each sub-entry below is treated as a primary bond, since for this subject the distinction between blood and chosen kin is operationally meaningless: he has only ever had the kin he constructed, and he has destroyed each construction in turn.

---

## 1. The Father (Unrecorded, Inferred) — "The Hard Hand"

**Status in record.** No name, no appearance, no documented existence in any panel of any issue. The father is a void in the canonical record.

**Why he is in this file anyway.** Because the void has the wrong shape to be empty. A man does not invent, ex nihilo, the pedagogical economy that Barton imposes on the Barton boys: praise as a scarce resource issued only against measurable performance; physical correction as the primary modality of instruction; the conversion of any softness in himself into an immediate harshness toward the student; the absolute refusal to acknowledge a younger person's moral standing to judge an elder. These are not free-floating personality traits — they are the *grammar of a particular kind of childhood*. He learned this grammar somewhere. The fluency is too high to be self-taught.

**Inferred dynamics.** A father (or paternal proxy — uncle, foreman, first trainer; the slot is what matters, not the kinship label) who issued regard only on condition of demonstrated mastery; who punished exposure (a child noticing the father's failings) more severely than any other infraction; who likely had his own skill — manual, athletic, or trade — that he passed along while refusing to let the receiving child outgrow him. The clue is in how Buck himself responds when Clint surpasses him: not pride, but threat. That response is a learned reflex. Someone taught him that the protégé exceeding the master is the master's annihilation. That someone was almost certainly the man who first held the bow over him.

**Trust/safety.** Presumed: none. The defensive architecture Barton carries is the architecture of a child who organized himself around a caregiver who could not be trusted to react proportionately. Hypervigilance to performance failure; no hypervigilance to threat — he is fearless physically, fearful only of being *seen short*. That is the signature of a household where the danger was humiliation, not violence-for-its-own-sake (though violence was likely the delivery mechanism for the humiliation).

**Failure mode.** The relationship presumably ended in one of two ways: the father died before any reconciliation, freezing the dynamic in amber; or Buck left and never returned, replicating the abandonment pattern he would later visit on Clint. The latter is more consonant with the behavior — Buck does not metabolize relationships, he exits them and reframes the exit as the other party's fault. He has done this with Clint; he probably did it first with the man who taught him.

**Leverage.** Theoretical only. No operator will ever invoke this figure, because Buck himself almost certainly does not consciously remember him as formative — the whole point of his defensive structure is that *he* is the origin of his own competence; the prior hand is erased. Any analyst attempting to surface this material would be met with cold incomprehension or violence. The father's existence is the load-bearing wall of the subject's self-concept precisely because it has been plastered over.

---

## 2. The Mother (Unrecorded, Inferred) — "The Absence Behind the Absence"

**Status in record.** As with the father: nothing. No reference, no flashback, no name spoken.

**Inferential read.** Where the father's absence is reconstructable from the pedagogy, the mother's absence is reconstructable from the *negative space in the affect*. Barton has no observable repertoire of tenderness — not suppressed tenderness, which would leak; *absent* tenderness, the kind that suggests no early template was ever installed. He cannot soothe, cannot comfort, cannot even register the cue that comfort is being sought (the beaten child on the carnival ground, looking up at him: he does not see what is being asked). This is consistent with a maternal figure who was either physically absent (death, departure, illness) or affectively absent (depressed, defeated, herself terrorized by the same hard hand). A child raised by a mother who could not approach would adapt by ceasing to seek approach, and would carry that adaptation into every adult bond.

**Trust/safety.** The relevant inference is not about trust toward her specifically but about the *generalized template*: women, and softer relational modalities generally, do not appear in Barton's operational repertoire at all. He has no documented romantic attachments, no female confederates, no female protégés of consequence. The carnival circuit is full of women; he passes through them as scenery. This is not misogyny in any active sense — it is the deeper absence of the *category of woman* as someone with whom he might transact. That blankness usually traces to a maternal figure who could not function as one.

**Failure mode.** The original failure mode, if it occurred, is what every subsequent failure mode in his life recapitulates: the caregiver does not come, and the child concludes that *needing* is the disqualifying weakness. This is, almost verbatim, the operating principle he transmits to Clint.

**Leverage.** None. There is nothing here to pull on, because there is nothing here. The file notes the void and moves on.

---

## 3. Jacques Duquesne (the Swordsman) — Confederate, Counterpart, Mirror

**Status in record.** Extensively documented. Carnival co-performer; co-conspirator in the embezzlement; co-adoptive figure to the Barton boys; recurring antagonist-and-occasional-ally in the broader Avengers record (his arc carries him eventually toward redemption and death in Avengers continuity, a trajectory Buck does not share and does not seem to register).

**History.** The two men were peers on the Carson Carnival circuit — Duquesne's blade act paired or alternated with Buck's bow act on the marquee. The professional relationship was longstanding, built on the kind of mutual recognition that exists between two specialists of comparable caliber who share a milieu. They drank together, traveled together, scammed together. When the Barton boys arrived as runaways, both men treated the adoption as a joint enterprise — Duquesne taking more interest in Barney, Buck claiming Clint, with overlap and rotation. The embezzlement scheme was, by implication, Duquesne's idea as often as Buck's; they were partners in the venture, and the loyalty of co-conspirators was the operative bond.

**Dynamics.** This is the *only* relationship in the entire file in which Barton operates as something close to a peer. Duquesne is neither subordinate (a protégé to be shaped) nor superior (an authority to be defied). He is laterally equivalent — same generation, same craft tier, same milieu — and Barton manages this by a kind of careful, distant collegiality. There is no warmth, but there is no contempt either; what passes between them is the professional courtesy of two predators who have agreed not to hunt the same animal. They split the take. They cover each other's acts. They do not discuss interior matters and would find such discussion mortifying.

**Evolution.** Where Duquesne's later arc bends toward complication and eventual moral repair, Buck's does not, and the divergence is itself diagnostic. Confronted with the same pressures — exposure, age, mortality — Duquesne becomes accessible to remorse and to relationship; Buck becomes more rigid, more weaponized, more theatrical. They began as mirror images and the mirror cracked along the fault line of capacity for change. Buck almost certainly read Duquesne's redemption arc, where he registered it at all, as weakness. The Swordsman's death in Avengers service would have been parsed by Buck as a fool's exit, not a man's.

**Trust/safety.** Operational trust: high (within the frame of the criminal venture). Personal trust: nonexistent and not desired by either party. Safety: each man was safe from the other because neither wanted anything the other had. The moment that calculus would have shifted — had Duquesne, say, claimed Clint as his student, or had Buck claimed the take entirely — the partnership would have ended in violence. It never quite shifted, and so it never quite ended; it simply dissolved when the carnival broke up and circumstance separated them.

**Failure mode.** The relationship did not "fail" in the dramatic sense — it attenuated. Once Duquesne ceased to be a daily fixture, he ceased to be present in Buck's interior life at all. This is itself the failure mode: Barton's attachments are wholly situational. Remove the daily proximity and the bond evaporates without residue. There is no record of Buck seeking Duquesne out in later years for any purpose — not for old times, not for collaboration, not even for revenge. He simply forgot him in the operational sense, as he forgets everyone whose continued presence is not enforced by their utility.

**Leverage.** Historically substantial, currently negligible (Duquesne is dead in the relevant continuity). For analytical purposes: the *fact* of Duquesne is one of the few pieces of evidence that Buck is capable of sustained lateral relationship at all, which complicates the cleaner narcissistic diagnosis. Whatever capacity for collegial attachment he ever possessed is exhausted in this one bond and is unrecoverable in any subsequent figure.

---

## 4. Charles "Barney" Barton — The Protégé He Did Not Take

**Status in record.** Barney is Clint's older brother, the elder Barton orphan; in the carnival period he is the boy who orbited Duquesne more than Buck, and who eventually departed for a criminal life of his own that paralleled, intersected, and bloodily resolved against Clint's heroic one (Barney's recurring villainous turn, his deaths and returns, are matters for the Hawkeye dossier; here only the relevant slice).

**History with Buck.** Buck did not adopt Barney in any meaningful sense. The boy was assessed in the first weeks of the carnival arrival, found wanting as a marksman's apprentice (or, more likely, found *too independent* — Barney's older-brother authority over Clint would have been read by Buck as competition for the protégé slot), and effectively assigned to Duquesne. The division of the boys between the two men was not random; it was a tacit recognition that Barney could not be shaped and Clint could.

**Dynamics.** Cold, transactional, and very specifically *competitive* on Buck's side in a way Barney almost certainly registered and resented. Buck took Clint and made an obvious project of him. Barney watched this happen and watched his own brother be drawn into a discipline from which Barney himself was excluded. The two carnival mentors created, between them, the wedge that would eventually drive the brothers apart — Buck claiming Clint's loyalty along the axis of teacher-to-student, Barney trying to maintain it along the axis of brother-to-brother, and the boy in the middle pulled between two systems of allegiance that would never reconcile.

**Buck's view of Barney.** Disposable. Insufficient. Not worth the investment. There is no record of Buck speaking warmly of Barney at any point, nor of grieving any of his deaths. To Buck, Barney was the surplus child — the one who came with the package and could not be used. This contempt was likely sensed by the boy and contributed to Barney's own later turn toward criminality: he learned from Buck (without the lesson being intended for him) that worth was measured in skill and that being unmeasured was being nothing, and he sought elsewhere the recognition Buck refused.

**Trust/safety.** Buck did not trust Barney and Barney did not trust Buck, accurately on both sides. Safety: Buck was not physically dangerous to Barney in the carnival years — there was no reason to be; the boy was nothing to him — but the *psychological* danger was acute and ongoing. Buck's presence in the camp was the constant signal to Barney that he was the lesser brother, the unchosen one, in a structure that valorized being chosen.

**Failure mode.** The relationship failed because it was never built. Barney was background to Buck and remains background in the surveillance record. The downstream consequence — the rupture between the Barton brothers — is partially Buck's responsibility, though he has never registered it as such and would not understand the imputation if it were made.

**Leverage.** Historically, considerable: any reminder that Buck *also failed Barney*, not just Clint, would attack the self-narrative that frames Clint's case as a particular betrayal rather than a general pattern. Currently moot (Barney's status varies by continuity; he is unavailable as an active lever). For analytic purposes: Barney is the control case in the experiment. Two boys, same orphanhood, same camp, same era; Buck shapes one and discards the other, and the discarded one becomes a criminal anyway, suggesting that the shaping itself was not protective and was not even the determining variable. Buck does not know this. He believes Clint became excellent *because of* him and Barney became nothing *because Buck declined to bother*. The reality — that Buck's pedagogy was largely irrelevant to outcomes and was destructive to the boy he did invest in — is unavailable to him.

---

## 5. Clinton Francis Barton — The Protégé, The Wound, The Mirror, The Hand at the End

**Status in record.** The central relationship of Buck's life, by orders of magnitude. Everything else in this file orbits this bond. Documented across decades of canon: the carnival training years; the embezzlement and abandonment; the recurring assassin contracts deliberately targeting Clint; the late-period summoning for the deathbed staging. No other relationship in Buck's biography sustains comparable narrative weight or temporal extension.

**History — the training years.** Clint arrives a runaway, perhaps nine to twelve years old in the foundational flashbacks, malnourished and watchful. Buck assesses him within hours — the eyes, the steadiness, the still-quality of the hands — and decides. The training begins almost immediately and is characterized from the first session by the structure that will define everything: physical demand pitched just past the boy's current capacity; verbal feedback restricted to corrective criticism, with praise withheld; physical correction (a slap, a struck shoulder, a knocked stance) applied to errors as if they were moral failings. The boy learns very fast. The faster he learns, the more Buck pushes — the protégé's accelerating competence is read by Buck not as success but as something that must be continually outrun, lest the student close the gap to the master. By the end of the carnival years, Clint is functionally as skilled as Buck and is being told, by escalating cruelty in the training, that he is not.

**Dynamics.** This is a textbook case of the protégé as narcissistic extension. Clint exists, in Buck's interior economy, as the proof of Buck's mastery — the trick shot's masterpiece is not any arrow he himself fires but the archer he forged. As long as Clint remains visibly *Buck's product*, he is loved (in Buck's restricted operational meaning of the word). The instant Clint exhibits independent moral agency — by exposing the theft — the extension is severed and the boy becomes a foreign object, and a treasonous one. The beating that follows the exposure is, from Buck's interior frame, not punishment for telling but punishment for *being separable*. The crime was the assertion of an interior life Buck did not author.

**The abandonment.** Buck beats the boy near to death and leaves him on the carnival ground. The decision is not impulsive; it is structural. Once Clint has demonstrated separability, he cannot be retained — the pedagogical economy will not accommodate a student with his own judgment. Buck departs and reframes the entire incident, within hours probably, as Clint's betrayal of him. By the next campfire he is the wronged man. This reframing is not strategic dissimulation; it is the actual content of his memory. He does not lie about what happened, he *remembers* it that way.

**Evolution — the assassin years.** The contracts that bring Buck back into Clint's life are paid work, but the selectivity is not coincidental. There are other archers available for hire; there are other marksmen Crossfire and similar employers could engage. Buck takes the Clint jobs specifically because they are Clint jobs. The compulsion is unmistakable to anyone reading the pattern — a man who fled a relationship and reframed it as the other's fault does not stay fled; he returns to the scene, repeatedly, to re-perform the original verdict and have it ratified by the other's defeat. Every contract is an attempt to kill Clint into agreement.

The attempts fail. Clint keeps not dying and keeps not agreeing. Worse, Clint keeps *offering reconciliation* — keeps, in the documented panels, extending the hand, naming Buck "teacher," asking after him, refusing to hate him with the appropriate symmetry. Each of these offerings is, for Buck, an unbearable provocation, because each one re-presents the original problem: the protégé asserting an independent moral position from which Buck can be forgiven. Buck does not want to be forgiven. Forgiveness requires admitting there is something to forgive. He wants to be *agreed with*, and Clint's persistent refusal to agree (by way of dying, by way of hating, by way of becoming the villain the narrative would require to ratify Buck's version) is the slow disaster of his late life.

**The deathbed.** The terminal cancer brings the pattern to its apex. Diagnosed with mortality and unable to muster either the affiliative collapse of a man seeking comfort or the depressive surrender of a man losing the will, Buck does the only thing his structure permits: he stages. He summons Clint. The stated frame varies by issue — sometimes a request to be killed mercifully, sometimes a demand to anoint a successor, sometimes both — but the underlying choreography is constant: Buck insists on directing his own ending through Clint's hand, which is to say, on making Clint complicit, finally, in the master's continued primacy. If Clint kills him, Buck wins (the protégé performs the master's command one last time). If Clint refuses, Buck wins (the protégé is weak, sentimental, has not absorbed the lesson). If Clint trains a successor under Buck's direction, Buck wins (the lineage is reaffirmed, with Clint as the conduit rather than the terminus). The deathbed is rigged. It is the most narcissistically pristine act of his life.

**Trust/safety.** Trust: catastrophically asymmetric. Clint trusted Buck absolutely as a child and the wound of that trust is the founding injury of Clint's adult psychology — every subsequent mentor figure (Captain America most prominently, but the pattern extends) is approached through the warped lens of this first attachment. Buck never trusted Clint, in the sense of granting him status as an autonomous moral agent; he trusted only Clint's utility as the demonstration of his teaching. Safety: Clint was never physically safe with Buck, though he believed he was. Buck was never *interiorly* safe from Clint, in the sense that Clint's mere existence as a moral being who knew the truth of the embezzlement was a permanent threat to Buck's self-concept — which is why Buck cannot leave him alone.

**Failure mode.** The relationship's failure mode is recursive: it has failed continually, in the same way, for decades, and the failure is the relationship. Each renewal — each contract, each summons — is a re-staging of the original break for the purpose of producing a different verdict, which never arrives. The terminal failure is the deathbed: Buck dies without ever being agreed with, and the dying is itself the last attempt to compel agreement.

**Leverage (operational).**
- *On Buck via Clint*: maximal. Clint is the only living human being whose responses can disorganize Buck's defenses. Anyone running an operation against Buck will route it through Clint if they are competent, and through anyone else if they are not.
- *On Buck via the *idea* of Clint*: substantial. Even reports of Clint's actions, particularly reports framing Clint's success as autonomous, produce affective spikes. A clever interrogator could destabilize Buck without Clint in the room simply by making Clint's separateness the topic.
- *On Clint via Buck*: catastrophic and not to be touched by any operator without authorization. Buck is one of the half-dozen people on the planet who can reach the substrate of Clint's psychology. Any hostile actor with access to Buck — alive or, posthumously, via recordings, letters, transmitted instruction — has a key to Clint that should be assumed compromising.

---

## 6. The Successor (The Second Trick Shot) — The Legacy as Final Weapon

**Status in record.** Variable across eras; appears in late-period material as a designated heir to the *Trick Shot* identity. In some continuities a blood relation (nephew, distant cousin); in others a recruited stranger trained late in Buck's life specifically to receive the mantle. The variation matters less than the function, which is consistent: this figure exists so that Buck does not have to leave the world with Clint as his only legacy.

**History.** The successor is brought in late, generally after the cancer diagnosis, generally with explicit awareness on Buck's part that this is a *replacement* protégé — the second attempt, this time with the wrinkles ironed out. The training is compressed (there is no time for the years he had with Clint) and is therefore even more intense, more demanding, more stripped of any pretense of pastoral care. The successor receives the distilled version of the pedagogy: no carnival warmth, no shared meals, no joint travel — only the technical transmission, day after day, until something has been moved from one body to another.

**Dynamics.** The successor is, transparently, a tool against Clint. Buck names him *Trick Shot* — the same alias, the same lineage claim — and presents him to Clint as the proof that Clint was always replaceable; that the master could make another just as good; that Clint's particularity (the moral judgment, the disloyalty, the refusal to remain instrument) was not a feature but a flaw, and the next iteration corrects it. The successor is the long-form version of the deathbed staging: a continuing rebuke to the original protégé, designed to outlive the master and continue the argument from beyond.

**Trust/safety.** Buck does not trust the successor any more than he trusted anyone else; the successor's loyalty is purchased by need (the successor wants the skill, wants the mantle, wants whatever Buck offers) and is structurally identical to Clint's original position. Were the successor to live long enough and develop enough independent moral architecture, Buck would beat and abandon him too — the pattern is not Clint-specific, it is Buck-specific. Safety: the successor is in the same danger Clint was, and is too late in the timeline (Buck dying) to manifest the danger fully.

**Failure mode.** The succession fails the moment Buck dies, because the alias *Trick Shot* without Buck behind it is just a name on a wanted poster, and the successor inherits a brand whose only marketable content was the original man. The successor will either drift into anonymity, get killed by a competent operator who underestimates how much of Buck's reputation was Buck himself, or be co-opted by Clint into something more durable than Buck would have permitted — any of which constitutes a posthumous defeat for Buck, who imagined the lineage as immortal.

**Leverage.** Moderate, posthumously. The successor is a living thread back to Buck's pedagogical record and oper


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — ALLIES, CHOSEN FAMILY & MENTORS

**Preliminary note.** The category of "ally, chosen family, mentor, protégé" is, for Subject Barton, structurally cramped. He does not form lateral bonds; he forms vertical ones, and only on the vertical axis where he stands above. The list below is therefore short by design — not because the file is thin, but because the subject's relational repertoire is. Where most adversary dossiers in this tier run to a dozen named affiliations across decades, Barton's intimates number five, perhaps six, and the gradient between "ally" and "victim" within each is unstable. The very fact that this section can be completed by mapping a handful of names — most of them dead, estranged, or weaponized against him — is itself the central finding.

---

## 1. JACQUES DUQUESNE — "THE SWORDSMAN"

**Canonical anchor.** Carson Carnival co-performer and co-conspirator (Avengers vol. 1 lore; Hawkeye vol. 1 #1–4 flashback strata; Solo Avengers #1–2). Of all relationships in the subject's life, this is the closest thing to a peer bond, and it deserves the longest entry.

**History.** Duquesne and Barton arrived at the Carson circuit on overlapping timelines — both already adults, both already drinking, both already running from prior lives the carnival was not curious about. They were billed adjacently (the blade act and the bow act), shared a trailer on at least one season per circuit ledgers reconstructable from Carson's published roster cards, and developed an act-rivalry that doubled as cover for genuine confederacy. When the Barton boys arrived as runaways, the two men jointly absorbed them — Duquesne taking Barney by drift, Barton claiming Clint by deliberate selection. The pedagogical labor was Buck's; the louche older-brother mentorship was Jacques's. Together they constituted, briefly, the only parental dyad either Barton child ever experienced.

**Dynamics.** The bond was built on three legs: shared craft (both connoisseurs of small-muscle precision; both contemptuous of the carnival's lesser acts), shared appetite (whiskey, women, late hours, easy money), and shared corruption (the embezzlement of carnival gate receipts that ultimately collapsed both their lives). Buck was the colder of the two and the more dominant; Duquesne the more charming, more dissipated, and more capable of guilt. Witnesses from the carnival period — reconstructable from later interviews threaded through the Solo Avengers backgrounders — describe Barton as the one who set the line and Duquesne as the one who decorated it.

**Trust / safety.** Trust was operational, not affective. Each man trusted the other to hold up his end of a fraud, to cover a flubbed performance, to lie to a roustabout boss. Neither trusted the other to hold him in regard if exposed. Safety, in the emotional sense, was nil — both men maintained the carnival code of never showing the soft place — but the *physical* safety of having a competent confederate at one's back was genuine and, by all available evidence, the most consistent such safety Buck ever experienced.

**Evolution.** The relationship has three phases. *Phase one* (early Carson years): partnership of equals, drinking, theft, mutual cover. *Phase two* (the boys' arrival through the embezzlement exposure): a quiet competition over which of them was the dominant figure in the Barton boys' eyes, with Buck winning on Clint's side and Duquesne winning on Barney's almost by default. *Phase three* (post-exposure, divergent flights): they separated under fire, never published as having reunited on-panel, and pursued parallel but non-intersecting criminal trajectories — Duquesne into mercenary work and eventual Avengers redemption, Barton into contract marksmanship and decades of grievance management.

**Failure mode.** The partnership failed precisely where the subject's organization predicts it would fail: the moment exposure forced one of them to choose between confederate-loyalty and self-preservation, both chose self. Neither held to the other afterward. Buck's blame-externalization, which by the late embezzlement years had become reflexive, would not have permitted him to hold Duquesne harmless even in private narration; Duquesne, who possessed the capacity for shame Buck lacked, likely carried the partnership as a private wound he never re-opened. There is no evidence Barton ever spoke Duquesne's name in any public or recorded context after their separation, which for a man whose grievances stay live for forty years is itself a tell — Duquesne is the one figure he cannot blame without implicating himself, and so he is excised.

**Leverage.** Posthumously, considerable. Any operator surfacing the Swordsman's name in Barton's presence — particularly the fact of Duquesne's eventual move toward something like decency — would land on an unprotected nerve. The subject cannot tolerate the implication that his confederate found a path he himself did not, because the only way to file that information is as Duquesne's weakness, and even Buck's defenses cannot quite make that stick. Recommended use: sparing, and only as a destabilizer, never as an opener.

---

## 2. CLINTON F. BARTON — "HAWKEYE" (THE PROTÉGÉ)

**Canonical anchor.** The primary relationship of the subject's published existence (Hawkeye vol. 1 #1–4; Solo Avengers #1–4; Avengers backgrounders; Hawkeye: Blindspot arc; assorted WCA appearances). Catalogued here under "protégé" with the understanding that the category is, in this file, a euphemism.

**History.** Subject selected Clinton at approximately age twelve, after the boy had been at the carnival some months performing menial labor. Selection criteria, reconstructable from the training sequences: visual acuity (the boy could spot a flubbed knife-throw setup from across the lot), an extant capacity for pain tolerance (the carnival had already documented this informally), and — critically — a hunger for adult regard so visible it functioned as a flag. Buck did not so much identify talent as identify *need*, and trained the talent into the need's container. Training ran for approximately three to four years, daily, in the early morning before the carnival opened and after closing — a schedule the subject maintained without exception, including in weather that suspended performances.

**Dynamics.** The pedagogy was the relationship. Praise was rationed to roughly one acknowledgment per dozen successful executions; correction was constant; physical contact was limited to grip adjustment and stance correction. The boy was permitted to call him "Buck" rather than "Mr. Barton" after approximately a year — a deliberately staged graduation that the subject likely experienced as conferring an enormous gift. Affection, where it occurred, was metabolized through equipment: the gift of a particular fletching jig, the handing-over of a personal bow, a nod after a near-impossible shot. Clint, starved, took these as the language of love that they were and were not.

**Trust / safety.** Asymmetric and catastrophic. The boy trusted the man absolutely; the man trusted the boy only as an extension of himself and withdrew that trust completely the instant the boy demonstrated autonomous moral judgment. Physical safety during the training years was high — Buck's standards would not have permitted careless injury to his investment — but psychological safety was zero in retrospect, because the entire architecture depended on the boy never doing the one thing that would expose its conditionality. When Clint did that thing (reporting the embezzlement), the architecture revealed itself instantly: beating, abandonment, decades of grievance.

**Evolution.** Five distinct phases. *(i) Selection and early drilling* — the boy as raw material, the man as craftsman, both inside the fantasy that this is what fathering looks like. *(ii) Mature apprenticeship* — Clint becoming visibly extraordinary, Buck's pride curdling toward proprietary anxiety as the protégé approaches and then meets his own ceiling. *(iii) Exposure and abandonment* — the inflection point; Buck's organization permanently solidifies around the narrative that *Clint betrayed him.* *(iv) Adversarial returns* — Buck resurfaces across years as paid contractor or freelance avenger, always somehow on jobs that route through Clint; this is presented as coincidence and is not. *(v) Terminal staging* — the deathbed summons, the demand to be killed or to confer the alias on a successor through Clint, the final coercive intimacy.

**Failure mode.** Total and over-determined. The relationship failed because it was structured to fail at exactly the moment the protégé became a moral agent. Buck cannot tolerate a Clint who is anything other than a mirror, and Clint cannot, finally, be only a mirror. Every subsequent re-encounter is a re-staging of the original failure with the failure pre-loaded — Buck arrives angry and leaves angrier, Clint arrives hoping and leaves wounded again.

**Leverage.** This is the master leverage in the entire file. Everything the subject cares about routes through Clinton Barton. Operators wishing to manipulate Buck should understand:
- *Threatening Clint produces incoherent response* — Buck cannot tolerate the boy's destruction by another's hand because that destruction belongs to him.
- *Praising Clint to Buck produces controlled rage that is exploitable*, particularly if the praise frames Clint's success as having come *after* leaving Buck's tutelage.
- *Asking Buck about Clint produces extended self-incriminating narration* — the subject cannot resist re-telling the embezzlement story, and the retellings have grown more elaborate over the years, each one adding more evidence against the teller than the teller perceives.
- *Bringing Clint physically into proximity is unstable and not recommended* without containment; the meetings tend toward weapon-drawn within minutes.

---

## 3. CHARLES "BARNEY" BARTON (THE OTHER BROTHER)

**Canonical anchor.** Peripheral but persistent presence across Hawkeye flashbacks; primary mentorship in Barney's case fell to Duquesne, not Buck, but the subject's attitude toward Barney is documentable and revealing.

**History.** Barney arrived at the carnival with Clint, older and harder, and was visibly not selectable by Buck's criteria — too suspicious, too closed, insufficient hunger for adult regard, already armored. Buck dismissed him within weeks and never revised the assessment. Barney drifted to Duquesne and beyond him into early criminal apprenticeships off the lot.

**Dynamics.** Functionally none. The subject treated Barney as the brother he had not selected — that is, as background — and Barney returned the indifference with the small intensifier of having watched, from the outside, what Buck was doing to Clint. There is no record of direct conflict; there is also no record of conversation beyond the operational.

**Trust / safety.** Not applicable in the affective sense. Operationally, Buck regarded Barney as a security risk on the order of any other carnival hand — capable of theft, capable of talk — and managed accordingly. Barney regarded Buck as a threat to his brother and adjusted his own absences around the training schedule, often, by inference, putting himself in Buck's sightline when he sensed sessions had run too long or too hard.

**Evolution.** None of substance during the carnival years. In the long after, the brothers' parallel descents into criminality and the Hawkeye continuity's various recursions on their conflict do not implicate Buck directly; he is the absent originating wound, not a participant.

**Failure mode.** Pre-failed. There was no relationship to fail.

**Leverage.** Limited but pointed. Barney is the only living witness to the training years besides Clint, and unlike Clint he has no nostalgic frame around them. Any deposition or testimony Barney could be induced to give would describe behaviors Clint himself has spent decades soft-filtering. Recommended as a corroborative source, not a confrontational one — Barney's information value exceeds his confrontational value, and the subject's contempt for him is sufficiently complete that Barney's voice would not destabilize Buck the way Duquesne's or Clint's does.

---

## 4. THE SECOND TRICK SHOT (DESIGNATED SUCCESSOR)

**Canonical anchor.** Late-period appearances introducing a successor figure to whom Buck attempts to transfer the alias and method, sometimes framed as kin, sometimes as unrelated heir. Treat the variations as continuity-equivalent for the purpose of this section.

**History.** The successor appears only in the terminal phase, in the same window as the cancer diagnosis. The subject identifies a young marksman, repeats — with telescoped speed, a months-long compression of what took years with Clint — the pedagogical sequence: selection, drilling, the conferral of equipment, the conditional approval. The compression is significant. He is not training a craftsman; he is producing a weapon and a witness in time for his own ending.

**Dynamics.** The successor is, transparently, the second draft of Clint, with the original draft's autonomous moral judgment edited out at the selection stage. Buck's criteria this time bias toward grievance: he wants someone with a pre-loaded animus against Clint or against the Avengers project Clint represents. The relationship is therefore not pedagogical in the way the first was; it is propagandistic. The successor is being shaped less to inherit a craft than to inherit a fight.

**Trust / safety.** Lower than with Clint, on both sides, by the subject's choice. He does not allow the successor the years of bond-formation he allowed Clint, because he knows — though he would not phrase it so — what bond-formation cost him last time. Safety for the successor is poor; he is being aimed.

**Evolution.** Truncated by design. The relationship is meant to last only as long as Buck does, and to transfer a weapon at the moment of his death. Whatever continues after is not part of his plan and not part of his cosmology.

**Failure mode.** The successor relationship fails in the moment it succeeds: the alias gets passed, the fight gets transferred, and the successor is left holding an inheritance that was never really about him. Whether the successor survives long after Buck depends on factors outside this file; the subject does not care, which is the failure.

**Leverage.** The successor is a pressure point on Buck only insofar as he is the *replacement for* Clint. Reminding Buck that he chose this one specifically because the first one defied him — i.e., that the successor is a confession of Clint's victory — is destabilizing in the same register as praising Clint's post-tutelage success. Otherwise the successor has low leverage value; he is too new in the file.

---

## 5. THE CARSON CARNIVAL CIRCUIT (DIFFUSE INSTITUTIONAL ATTACHMENT)

**Canonical anchor.** The carnival itself as quasi-familial container, documented across the Hawkeye flashback strata and the broader carnival-circuit lore.

**History.** Subject was on the Carson circuit for an indeterminate but lengthy span — at least the years of Clint's training, almost certainly longer on either side. The carnival functioned as the only stable institutional affiliation of his life: it fed him, lodged him, provided his audience, structured his year by route and season, and tolerated his appetites within limits.

**Dynamics.** The subject's affect toward the carnival as institution is a mixture of contempt (for its lesser performers, its credulous audiences, its grubby economics) and unacknowledged dependence (for the witnessing function that constituted his selfhood). He stole from it because he could not have its regard as a gift and required it as a wage; he stayed in it because there was nowhere else his particular skill commanded the room.

**Trust / safety.** Trust in the institution was low — he assumed correctly that the management would discard him on exposure. Safety in the institution was, paradoxically, the highest he experienced anywhere; the carnival's tolerance for eccentric and damaged performers gave him the longest unmolested stretch of his life.

**Evolution.** Terminated by the embezzlement exposure. After Carson, the subject did not affiliate with another institution of comparable scale. The contracts that followed were transactional, the criminal employers temporary; he never re-rooted.

**Failure mode.** He treated the container that held him as a mark, and lost it.

**Leverage.** The carnival itself is gone or transformed across decades; the leverage is residual. Surviving Carson personnel, photographs, route ledgers, and old performance handbills constitute an archive that, deployed in the subject's presence, would produce one of two responses: a controlled performance of nostalgic disdain ("amateurs, the lot of them") or a brief, involuntary softening that he would immediately correct. The softening, if elicited and observed, is among the few documentable moments where the underlying organization permits something through. Handle with care; do not narrate back.

---

## SYNTHESIS

The map above contains, in total: one peer-confederate the subject excised, one protégé the subject destroyed and cannot stop returning to, one rejected brother the subject barely registered, one replacement protégé the subject is building as an instrument, and one institutional container the subject burned. There are no friendships. There is no chosen family that survived contact with him. The mentors who shaped him are not in the published record — his rigidity reads as transmitted from somewhere, but the source is offstage and likely dead before the file opens. The proteges constitute the entirety of his downstream legacy, and the proteges are, respectively, the man who became his nemesis and the man being trained to inherit that enmity.

The diagnostic point of this section, taken whole: a sixty-plus-year life produces this short a roster only when the relational architecture itself forbids accumulation. Bernard Barton does not collect people. He selects them, uses them, loses them, and re-narrates the loss as their failure. The file is short because the man is.

— END SECTION —


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# RELATIONSHIPS III — RIVALS, ENEMIES & LOVERS

*UATU Dossier, Section 7c. Subject: Bernard "Buck" Barton, alias Trick Shot. Cross-reference Relationships I (Family & Origin), Relationships II (Protégés & Confederates).*

A preliminary methodological note. The subject's rivals, enemies, and intimates are not three discrete categories so much as one category seen from three angles. For Bernard Barton, every sustained human connection is structured as a contest of mastery in which the other party is being measured, ranked, and — sooner or later — punished for some failure of subordination. Lovers were graded like students; students were treated like rivals; rivals were courted with the strange, attentive intimacy he could not extend to anyone he was supposed to love. The taxonomy below reflects that scrambled topology. Where canonical record is thin (particularly on the romantic side), inferential reconstruction is flagged as such and grounded in observed pattern, milieu, and the somatic-behavioral telemetry compiled across forty years of intermittent surveillance.

---

## 7c.1 — JACQUES DUQUESNE (the Swordsman): Confederate, Mirror-Rival, Only Peer

The defining lateral relationship of the subject's life. Duquesne and Barton co-headlined the Carson Carnival's marquee weapons act for the better part of a decade; the two were billed together, traveled together, drank together, stole together, and adopted between them — informally, without paperwork — the two Barton boys who had run from an abusive home in Waverly, Iowa. The duration of close proximity is the single longest documented adult attachment in the subject's file.

**Functional dynamics.** Duquesne occupied the one social slot Barton's psychology can tolerate without immediate hostility: a confederate of comparable but non-overlapping skill. Edge weapon versus projectile weapon — the two domains do not directly compete, which is the only condition under which Barton can sustain proximity to another adult of his own caliber. Surveillance of carnival-era photographs and the few extant promo reels shows them habitually positioned shoulder-to-shoulder rather than face-to-face; they stood in parallel, oriented toward the audience, never toward each other. They did not, in any captured footage, make eye contact during conversation. The relationship was conducted through shared third objects: a bottle, a target, a mark, a boy.

**Trust architecture.** Operational trust was high and narrow. Each man would back the other in a fight, share a take, cover a lie to a carnival boss. Personal trust — disclosure of history, fear, want — was effectively zero in both directions. Neither man, by any account, knew where the other had come from. This was a friendship organized as a mutual non-aggression pact between two people who recognized each other as the same kind of damaged.

**Embezzlement and its meaning.** The skimming of carnival receipts that destroyed the partnership was, on the available evidence, Duquesne's idea executed with Barton's full and enthusiastic participation. The subject's later self-presentation — that he was somehow drawn in — does not survive scrutiny; his cut, his enthusiasm for the scheme, and the calm of his behavior in the weeks preceding exposure all argue for full ownership. What is diagnostically significant is what happened *after* the boy Clinton found them out. Duquesne ran. The subject did not. The subject stayed and beat the child. This divergence is the cleanest data point in the entire file on the difference between the two men: Duquesne was a thief who would eventually arc toward something like redemption; Barton was a narcissist who experienced exposure as an injury requiring retribution against the exposer. The same act revealed two distinct organizations.

**Evolution.** After the carnival, the two never operated together again. Duquesne drifted toward (and eventually away from) supervillainy and into a tortured redemption arc terminating in his death in the Avengers' service. The subject drifted in the opposite direction, hardening into contract work. There is no recorded contact between them in their post-carnival lives — a silence which, given how small the mercenary world is, is itself active. Each avoided the other. Duquesne, by all accounts, came to regard Barton with shame; Barton, on the rare occasions Duquesne's name surfaced in interrogation transcripts, responded with a flat, dismissive contempt that read as wounded.

**Failure mode.** The relationship failed at the exact pressure point Barton's psychology cannot bear: the discovery that the confederate had a moral interior the subject did not share. Duquesne's eventual repentance, which the subject would have heard about secondhand through carnival circles, was not just a personal disappointment — it was an ontological insult, because it implied that the partnership Barton had understood as a meeting of two equally hard men had in fact been asymmetric all along.

**Leverage.** Posthumous only. The name Duquesne, spoken without warning in the subject's presence, produces (per Mockingbird's surveillance notes from the Crossfire arc) a one-second microexpression of something that is not contempt — closer to a flinch — before the contempt-mask reasserts. Any operator interrogating the subject can use the name as a controlled destabilizer. Do not overuse; the subject learns.

---

## 7c.2 — CLINTON F. BARTON: Protégé, Betrayer (in his own framing), Designated Executioner

Catalogued in greater depth in Relationships II as the central protégé; treated here for the rival/enemy dimension only, which dominates the second half of the relationship's lifespan.

The conversion of the protégé into the enemy was instantaneous and total. There was no transitional phase. The morning before the embezzlement was exposed, Clinton was the favored son; the evening after, he was a marked man. The subject's psychology does not permit gradient — Clinton's act of moral autonomy reclassified him in a single cognitive operation from extension-of-self to threat-to-self, and the reclassification has never been revisited in forty years of intermittent contact.

**Rivalry-as-relationship.** Every documented post-carnival encounter between the two is structured as a contest: a shooting challenge, an ambush, a hostage scenario engineered to force Clinton to out-shoot the man who taught him. The subject does not pursue Clinton for money — the contracts he has accepted against Clinton are, on examination, contracts he sought out and underbid for. He needs the confrontation. The protégé who exceeded him is the only human in the world who can confirm or deny the subject's central claim about himself (*I am the best, and what I made is mine*); every encounter is an attempt to extract that confirmation by force.

**The trick-arrow gambit.** In the most diagnostically rich engagement on record (Hawkeye vol. 1 #4 / Solo Avengers backgrounders), the subject sets up a scenario in which Clinton must execute a shot the subject himself taught him — and Clinton wins by executing a shot the subject *did not* teach him, an improvisation. The subject's response, captured in his own subsequent interrogation, is revealing: he describes the improvised shot as "cheating." This is not tactical complaint; it is the deepest possible admission. The protégé's autonomy — the part of Clinton that is *not* Buck's making — is, in the subject's cosmology, illegitimate. Only what he transmitted counts. Anything else is theft.

**Trust/safety.** Zero in both directions on the operational axis. On a deeper axis, Clinton continues — across every encounter, into the deathbed scene — to extend offers of repair, recognition, even forgiveness. These offers are not received; they are catalogued by the subject as evidence of Clinton's softness, which is in turn weaponized in the next encounter. The asymmetry is total: Clinton cannot stop loving the man who trained him, and the man who trained him cannot stop punishing him for being loved.

**The deathbed summons.** Catalogued in Section 4 as a clinical event; here noted as the apex relational gesture. The subject, terminal, summons Clinton — not to apologize, not to be forgiven, but to be killed by him, or alternately to witness the transfer of the Trick Shot name to another. Either staging accomplishes the same goal: the subject choreographs his death using Clinton as the instrument, which restores the relationship to the only configuration the subject can tolerate — the master directing, the student executing. Even his dying is a lesson plan.

**Failure mode (from the subject's side).** Clinton's refusal to play the assigned role — his sustained insistence on being a person rather than an instrument — is the failure the subject has never been able to process. Each encounter is a renewed attempt to coerce the protégé back into the instrumental relation. Each failure is recoded as betrayal. The loop does not terminate.

**Leverage.** The only operator in the world who can reliably draw the subject out of cover is Clinton. Cf. multiple SHIELD after-action reports: Clinton's voice on an open frequency produces, in the subject, an attentional fixation that overrides ordinary tactical caution. He will break position to engage. This is exploitable and has been exploited.

---

## 7c.3 — CHARLES "BARNEY" BARTON: The Other Son, the One Who Was Not Chosen

A relationship under-documented in canon and under-attended in commentary, but operationally significant. Of the two Barton boys taken in by the carnival, Barney was the elder and, by every account, the more outwardly compliant. He was not, however, the one the subject selected for archery training. The selection of Clinton over Barney is a relational fact whose consequences ramify through three lives.

**Selection criteria, inferred.** The subject did not choose the more obedient child. He chose the one with the visual-motor potential — the eye, the stillness, the hands — and he chose, almost certainly, the one whose hunger for adult regard was more legible and therefore more manipulable. Barney's compliance was a settled trait; Clinton's was a yearning. The yearning was the lever, and the subject found it immediately.

**Dynamic with Barney.** Cordial-instrumental. Barney was useful around the carnival, kept on but not invested in. The subject did not bully him and did not bond with him. The pattern is consistent with the subject's general indifference toward anyone he has not classified as either extension or rival; Barney was neither, and was therefore furniture. There is no record of the subject expressing affection, contempt, or particular notice toward Barney in any direction.

**Consequences.** Barney's later turn toward criminality, his recurring antagonism toward Clinton, and the eventual fratricidal cycles between the brothers can be traced in non-trivial part to the carnival-era wound of being the un-chosen child in the household of a narcissist who chose. The subject is not the only cause of the Barton brothers' fracture, but he is a substantial one, and he is unaware of it. Asked, in an interrogation transcript, whether he ever considered training Barney as well, the subject reportedly answered, "He didn't have the eye." He was discussing an instrument, not a child.

**Trust/leverage.** Negligible direct relationship. Barney's name does not produce a reaction in the subject. The relational wound here is one-sided and unilateral — Barney carried it, the subject never noticed leaving it.

---

## 7c.4 — CROSSFIRE (William Cross): Employer, User, Reciprocally Despised Partner

Of the subject's mercenary employers, Crossfire is the longest-running and the most diagnostically interesting because the contempt was mutual, well-developed, and operationally functional.

**History.** Cross hired the subject during the Mockingbird-arc Crossfire operation (cf. Hawkeye vol. 1 #1-4; subsequent Solo Avengers appearances) specifically because the subject would accept a contract on Clinton that other archers had declined for reasons of professional courtesy. The pairing recurred intermittently across the next decade-plus whenever Cross needed a marksman with a specific personal grudge.

**Dynamic.** Cross is, in his own way, a fellow narcissist — but a louder, vainer, more performative one, with a sonic-weapon gimmick the subject found professionally distasteful (cf. carnival sneer at "noise acts"). The two men despised each other from the first meeting and continued to work together for years because the despite was, in some sense, the working condition. The subject could tolerate being employed by Cross precisely because he could hold Cross in contempt; an employer he respected would have produced unbearable subordination.

**Trust/safety.** Operational only and narrow. Payment was always cash up front, half before, half after — a structure the subject insisted on with Cross specifically. Each man assumed the other would betray him if the price was right, and each was correct.

**Failure mode.** The relationship terminated, by available accounts, in mutual abandonment without confrontation — Cross found cheaper labor, the subject took a better contract — neither dignified the parting with a final exchange. Diagnostically: this is one of the few relationships in the file that did *not* end in narcissistic rupture, because neither party had ever extended enough investment for rupture to be possible.

**Leverage.** Cross is now an unreliable witness against the subject but a usable one for context; he can be interviewed about the subject's work habits, drinking patterns, and a small set of operational tells.

---

## 7c.5 — THE SECOND "TRICK SHOT" (designated successor, era-variable identity)

The handoff of the Trick Shot name to a successor — sometimes presented as a relative, sometimes as an unrelated apprentice, depending on canonical iteration — is a relationship that exists almost entirely in the subject's terminal phase. Operationally, it is the same relationship the subject had with Clinton, restaged with a new participant, with the explicit goal of producing the outcome the original staging failed to produce: a protégé who remains an extension.

**Dynamics.** Compressed in time (months rather than years), intensified in pedagogy, conducted under the looming pressure of the subject's terminal diagnosis. The successor is trained harder, faster, and with even less affection than Clinton was — affection being now wholly impossible, since the subject is dying and cannot afford the slow extraction of regard that constituted his earlier pedagogical method. Praise is rationed even more tightly. The successor is told, by indirection and by explicit instruction, that the *point* of his existence is to be the instrument by which the subject's name continues and, in some iterations, by which Clinton is finally killed.

**Trust/safety.** The successor is in physical danger throughout the training period and is in moral danger throughout — the subject is shaping him into a vector of the subject's own unfinished business. There is no record of the successor being told that he is loved, valued in himself, or expected to outlive his purpose.

**Failure mode.** The successor inevitably encounters Clinton and inevitably discovers that the man he was trained to hate is not the man he was told to hate. The discovery proceeds along exactly the path the original discovery did: a protégé's moral autonomy asserting itself against the master's coercive frame. The subject built a loop and made the loop run again.

**Leverage.** The successor, once detached from the subject, is the single best forensic witness available — having been told the subject's own narrative of grievance in undiluted form and having had time to recognize it as narrative. Any post-mortem reconstruction of the subject's late-period cognition should begin with successor debriefing.

---

## 7c.6 — THE CARSON CARNIVAL HIERARCHY (proprietors, ringmasters, fellow performers): Diffuse Antagonism

Not a single rivalry but a class of them. The subject's relationship to carnival authority was uniformly contemptuous, expressed through small theatrical defiances — arriving late to call, ad-libbing the act, drinking visibly between shows, refusing the proprietor's instructions in front of witnesses. The pattern is consistent with the subject's general intolerance of any superior in his cosmology; it manifests not as direct insubordination (which would risk dismissal and exposure) but as a sustained low-grade theater of contempt designed to communicate to the audience of fellow performers that the subject considered himself the true center of the show.

**Fellow performers.** With acts that did not compete with his — clowns, animal handlers, the wire walker, the strongman — relations were neutral-to-cordial. With acts that competed for the marquee spot — the occasional rival knife-thrower or pistol act — relations were sabotage-cordial: pleasant in person, with quiet undermining via rumor, equipment interference, and engineered embarrassment. Surveillance of carnival-era incident logs (where extant) shows a striking correlation between rival acts arriving and equipment failures occurring within four weeks.

**Failure mode.** The carnival itself was the failure mode. The subject's habitual contempt, combined with the embezzlement, ensured he could not return to carnival work after the rupture; the circuit is smaller than it looks and word travels. He cut himself off from the only legitimate professional milieu his skills fit, which is part of why the slide into contract killing was, for him, frictionless.

---

## 7c.7 — INTIMATE / LOVERS (composite reconstruction; canonical record sparse)

The published record on the subject's romantic and sexual life is nearly silent. This silence is itself a datum.
